Summary of Manufacturing AI Transformation (AX) Trend Analysis Results

The analysis of manufacturing AI transformation (AX) trends confirms that AI technology is being deeply integrated across the entire production process.
The most noteworthy areas, alongside advanced technologies such as 'In-situ quality monitoring' for real-time defect detection during additive manufacturing processes, are 'predictive maintenance' and 'AI vision inspection,' which still stand out as core application cases.
Furthermore, beyond individual technologies, approaches that innovate the entire system, such as 'Digital Twin' and 'AI-embedded manufacturing,' along with the systematic operation of AI models through MLOps, have emerged as important trends.
This demonstrates that AI is a core driver for maximizing quality and enhancing production systems, going beyond mere efficiency improvements.
However, ensuring compliance with GMP regulations and data integrity in the medical device sector are important challenges, and the establishment of new safety systems, such as AI-based virtual safety fences, is also being demanded.
For successful AX, a comprehensive strategy including data governance and regulatory compliance, in addition to technology adoption, is essential.

ë ˆì´ì € íŒŒìš°ë” ë² ë“œ ìœµí•©(Lâ€‘PBF)ì—ì„œ ê´‘í•™ í† ëª¨ê·¸ë˜í”¼(OT) ìŠ¤íŠ¸ë¦¼ì„ í™œìš©í•´ ë ˆì´ì–´ ì§„í–‰ ì¤‘ ê¸°ê³µ ê²°í•¨ì„ ì‹¤ì‹œê°„ íƒì§€í•˜ë©°, ìœˆë„ìš° í¬ê¸°(ì˜ˆ: WS=25)ë¡œ ì •í™•ë„â€‘ì§€ì—° íŠ¸ë ˆì´ë“œì˜¤í”„ë¥¼ ì¡°ì ˆí•´ ROCâ€‘AUC 0.72~0.99ì™€ ì¬ì½”íŒ… ì£¼ê¸°(â‰ˆ3ì´ˆ) ë‚´ íŒë‹¨(0.22~10.42s)ì„ ë‹¬ì„±í•©ë‹ˆë‹¤. ì´ë¥¼ í†µí•´ ê²°í•¨ íŒŒíŠ¸ë¥¼ ì¦‰ì‹œ ì¤‘ë‹¨(Stopâ€‘onâ€‘Defect)í•´ ìµœëŒ€ 15% ë¹„ìš© ì ˆê°ì´ ê°€ëŠ¥í•˜ë©°, ë‹¤í’ˆì¢… ë¹Œë“œì—ì„œëŠ” ê°ì²´ë³„ ìœˆë„ìš° ê´€ë¦¬ì™€ ì—£ì§€/ì˜¨í”„ë ˆë¯¸ìŠ¤ ë°°ì¹˜ë¥¼ ê¶Œì¥í•©ë‹ˆë‹¤. ì˜ì‚¬ì½”ë“œÂ·í•˜ì´í¼íŒŒë¼ë¯¸í„°ì™€ ê³µê°œ ë°ì´í„°ì…‹ì´ ì œê³µë˜ì–´ í˜„ì¥ PoCì™€ â€˜qualifyâ€‘asâ€‘youâ€‘buildâ€™ ì „í™˜ì„ ì‹ ì†íˆ ì¶”ì§„í•  ìˆ˜ ìˆìŠµë‹ˆë‹¤. By leveraging Optical Tomography (OT) streams in Laser Powder Bed Fusion (L-PBF), pore defects are detected in real-time during layer progression, and by adjusting the accuracy-latency tradeoff with window sizes (e.g., WS=25), ROC-AUCs of 0.72-0.99 and decisions (0.22-10.42s) within the recoating cycle (â‰ˆ3s) are achieved. This enables up to 15% cost savings by immediately stopping defective parts (Stop-on-Defect). For multi-part builds, object-specific window management and edge/on-premise deployment are recommended. Pseudocode, hyperparameters, and publicly available datasets are provided, allowing for rapid acceleration of on-site PoCs and a transition to 'qualify-as-you-build'.
ìš”ì•½:Summary:
ë³¸ ë…¼ë¬¸ì€ L-PBF ê³µì •ì—ì„œ OT ì„¼ì„œ ë°ì´í„°ë¥¼ ë ˆì´ì–´ ë‹¨ìœ„ë¡œ ì‹¤ì‹œê°„ ì²˜ë¦¬í•˜ì—¬ ê¸°ê³µ ìœ„í—˜ì„ ì¡°ê¸°ì— íŒë³„í•˜ëŠ” ìŠ¤íŠ¸ë¦¬ë° íŒŒì´í”„ë¼ì¸(Hephaestus)ì„ ì œì•ˆí•©ë‹ˆë‹¤. ê³µê°„ ì´ìƒì¹˜-ì§€ë¬¸í™”-ê¸°ê³„í•™ìŠµ(k-NN)ìœ¼ë¡œ êµ¬ì„±ë˜ë©°, ìœˆë„ìš° í¬ê¸°ì™€ ì „ì§„í­ì„ ì¡°ì ˆí•´ ì •í™•ë„(ROC-AUC ìµœëŒ€ 0.99)ì™€ ì§€ì—°(0.22~10.42ì´ˆ) ì‚¬ì´ë¥¼ ê³µì •ì ìœ¼ë¡œ ìµœì í™”í•  ìˆ˜ ìˆìŠµë‹ˆë‹¤. ì¬ì½”íŒ… ì£¼ê¸°(3ì´ˆ) ë‚´ ì‘ë‹µì„ ì¶©ì¡±í•˜ëŠ” ì„¤ì •(WS=25)ì—ì„œ ROC-AUC>0.8ì„ ë‹¬ì„±í–ˆê³ , ìŠ¤íŠ¸ë¦¬ë°í˜• ë¹„ìš© ëª¨ë¸ì„ í†µí•´ ìŠ¤í¬ë© ë¹„ìœ¨ì´ 20% ì´ìƒì´ë©´ ì¡°ê¸° ì¤‘ë‹¨ìœ¼ë¡œ ë¹„ìš© ì ˆê° íš¨ê³¼ê°€ í½ë‹ˆë‹¤. ì˜ì‚¬ì½”ë“œÂ·ë³µì¡ë„Â·íŒŒë¼ë¯¸í„°ì™€ ê³µê°œ ë°ì´í„°ì…‹ê¹Œì§€ ì œê³µë˜ì–´ í˜„ì¥ PoCì— ì¦‰ì‹œ í™œìš© ê°€ëŠ¥í•˜ë©°, SPE í†µí•©Â·ì¦ë¶„ì²˜ë¦¬Â·ë‹¤ì„¼ì„œ ìœµí•© ë° íë£¨í”„ ì œì–´ë¡œ í™•ì¥ ì‹œ ì œì¡° AX ê°€ì¹˜ê°€ ë”ìš± ì»¤ì§‘ë‹ˆë‹¤. This paper proposes a streaming pipeline (Hephaestus) that processes OT sensor data layer-by-layer in real-time in the L-PBF process to enable early detection of porosity risk. It is composed of spatial outlier detection, fingerprinting, and machine learning (k-NN), and can be optimally configured for the process by adjusting the window size and stride to balance between accuracy (ROC-AUC up to 0.99) and latency (0.22~10.42 seconds). In a configuration (WS=25) that meets the response time within the recoating cycle (3 seconds), an ROC-AUC > 0.8 was achieved, and through a streaming cost model, if the scrap rate exceeds 20%, early termination leads to significant cost savings. Pseudocode, complexity, parameters, and even a public dataset are provided, making it immediately usable for on-site PoC (Proof of Concept), and when extended to SPE (Stream Processing Engine) integration, incremental processing, multi-sensor fusion, and closed-loop control, the manufacturing AX value further increases.

The key to AI adoption in bio/pharmaceutical manufacturing is simultaneously establishing a high-quality data infrastructure and a regulatory compliance framework. This requires embedding the ALCOA principle into on-site procedures through the integration of sensors, SCADA, MES, LIMS, and by establishing standards, metadata, lineage, and governance. Deploy and monitor a portfolio of process- and variable-specific specialized models (rather than generic ones) at scale on an MLOps-based platform, and train and operate real-time large-volume data with a cloud-centric architecture for security, access, and traceability. Furthermore, by establishing quality and patient safety impact assessment, change management, and validation systems in accordance with FDA/EMA's risk-based guidance, compliance can be ensured.

Performance can be managed using FPY, scrap/rework rates, NC/CAPA/document approval lead times, and recall rates, allowing for simultaneous improvement in quality and productivity even amidst labor shortages.

In the medical device manufacturing industry, the adoption of AI is accelerating against the backdrop of labor shortages and increasing recalls, with core process automation, defect detection, document processing automation, and predictive analytics being key challenges. Still, paper-based QMS (17%) poses risks to quality, efficiency, and patient safety, and the transition to eQMS, along with the establishment of data and regulatory requirements (ISO 13485, 21 CFR Part 11, CSV), must precede. Practically, start with a pilot (8-12 weeks) for vision inspection and document automation, and integrate them with MES, QMS, etc., to secure traceability. Manage effects with KPIs such as FPY, CAPA lead time, document approval time, and recall rate, and through change management, systematizing the collaboration between on-site SMEs and data specialists, upskilling, and Human-in-the-loop operations is key to success.

The NAM roadmap emphasizes that the prerequisites for AI competitiveness are 'sufficient, affordable, and reliable power' and rapid permitting and infrastructure expansion. The cases of Belden, Accenture, and NVIDIA demonstrate how TSN-based deterministic networking time-synchronizes multiple cameras and sensors, enabling AI to detect worker entry into hazardous zones in real-time and immediately stop robots/AGVs, thereby reducing false positives and false negatives and increasing the reliability of safety interlocks. Virtual safety fences and an orchestration layer (integrating vision, sensors, robots, and safety PLCs, with policy-based stopping/deceleration/warning) complement the limitations of fixed guards and secure smart factory flexibility, such as for changeovers and line expansions. Maximizing ROI by combining safety monitoring and real-time quality inspection using the same infrastructure (cameras, edge, TSN), a practical approach involves SIL/PL alignment, latency budget, and failsafe design during implementation, followed by quantifying effectiveness through pilot projects in high-risk cells and using near-miss incidents and stop response time as metrics.

Belden, together with Accenture and NVIDIA, presented a physical AI safety system that combines a TSN-based 'virtual safety fence' with real-time quality inspection. Through multi-camera video synchronization, it accurately detects worker intrusion to automatically stop or decelerate robots, and by performing quality inspection on the same infrastructure, investment efficiency can be increased. Through IT/OT integrated orchestration, line deployment and expansion are easy, and it is useful in a retrofit manner for modernizing safety in existing processes and addressing labor shortages. Upon implementation, conformance to functional safety standards, latency budget and fail-safe design, security and data governance, and pilot verification and KPI management (reduction of near misses, stop response time, impact on operational availability) are key.

The core of defense and shipbuilding M&A is a manufacturing AX execution strategy that focuses on the internalization (vertical integration) of parts and materials and the expansion of global production bases, thereby reducing supply chain risks and enhancing cost and delivery competitiveness. For the defense industry, regulatory compliance capabilities such as export controls and cybersecurity must be internalized into due diligence and PMI, along with the acquisition/joint ventures of core technologies such as AI, robotics, unmanned systems, and satellites. For the shipbuilding industry, profitability for small and medium-sized companies must be pursued through securing overseas yards (modules/blocks), focusing on high-value-added processes domestically, and eco-friendly/retrofit and MRO services. Furthermore, leveraging digital and smart manufacturing capabilities, such as the standardization of MES, PLM, and quality data, and the design of KPIs for internalization rates, cost, and delivery, as a core leverage for PMI, and securing local partnerships/bases that comply with localization regulations (Buy American, local content) in countries like the US, is effective.

In the structural growth phase of the defense and shipbuilding industries, M&A serves as a means for vertical integration (internalization) and the expansion of global production bases. For the defense industry, acquiring and internalizing AI, robotics, unmanned, and satellite technologies, along with deal due diligence including technology protection and export controls, determines success or failure. The shipbuilding industry secures low-cost production capacity through overseas yards, while domestic operations focus on high-value-added processes; small and medium-sized players pursue stable profits through MRO and eco-friendly retrofits. To respond to US MASGA, local acquisitions and joint ventures equipped with localization and regulatory compliance capabilities are effective. From a Manufacturing AX perspective, the practical points are an internalization roadmap, digital integration (MES/PLM), KPI-based synergy management, AI and unmanned PoCs, and the establishment of security and regulatory governance.

Machina Labs provides its 'Elastic Factory,' which produces metal parts directly from CAD through AI-guided robotic forming without molds, significantly reducing initial CAPEX and lead time. In environments with high-mix, low-volume (HMLV) production and frequent design changes, it accelerates the transition from initial production to mass production and changeovers. For difficult-to-process materials such as high-temperature alloys and thin-walled/complex shapes, it enhances the balance between quality and cost as well as design freedom. It has been validated through cases with Toyota, Lockheed, and NASA for mass customization and MRO/rework in aerospace, defense, and automotive (including exterior panels). For adoption, it is effective to begin with a PoC for low-volume sheet metal, initial development, and MRO parts where mold costs are substantial, and to concurrently design a CAD-to-part digital thread and in-line measurement/traceability to build a data-driven flywheel for cycle time and precision improvement.

This reduces mold investment and change lead times, demonstrating strengths in low-volume, high-mix production, frequent design changes, and prototype-to-initial mass production transitions. The Europe AI 100 report systematically maps vendors (e.g., PhysicsX, NEURA Robotics, Tacto, Axelera AI) in key manufacturing AX domains such as robotics and autonomous systems, process/engineering simulation, supply chain and procurement AI, and chip/compute and governance, and presents vendor maturity and agility signals like lean organizations averaging 75 employees, UK/Germany/France hubs, and young founding years. Sifted AI 100 is a 'market map' that provides an overview of European AI startups by sector, which the manufacturing industry can leverage for vendor sourcing, partnership discovery, POC candidate selection, and AI infrastructure strategy formulation. Key takeaways: concentrated investment in robotics/industrial technology and dual-use sectors; accelerated process innovation with the emergence of simulation and new material AI; maturing infrastructure (compute, data, governance); and the formation of hubs centered in the UK, Germany, and France. Practical application: constructing a pilot lineup of 3-5 options for each area of interest such as robotics, simulation, supply chain, and governance; comparing the TCO, energy efficiency, and on-premise suitability of GPU/photonic and edge chips; generating initial savings with back-office agents in areas like procurement and finance; and proactively managing partner risk by using investor/funding size as a vendor stability indicator.

Reimagine PLM as a product-centric integrated architecture (digital thread/data fabric) rather than a single tool replacement. Prepare for AI/copilot to operate based on consistent metadata and governance, including EBOM-MBOM consistency, change history, and requirements/quality linkage. ì›ìì¬ ì¬ì£¼ë¬¸ì— AI ì—ì´ì „íŠ¸ë¥¼ ë„ì…í•˜ë©´ ì‹¤ì‹œê°„ ë°ì´í„°ì™€ ì˜ˆì¸¡ì„ í†µí•´ ê²°í’ˆÂ·ë‹¤ìš´íƒ€ì„ì„ ì„ ì œì ìœ¼ë¡œ ì¤„ì´ê³  ë¹„ìš©ì„ ì ˆê°í•˜ë©°, ì‚¬ëŒ-ì—ì´ì „íŠ¸ í˜‘ì—…ìœ¼ë¡œ ì˜ì‚¬ê²°ì •ì„ ê³ ë„í™”í•  ìˆ˜ ìˆìŠµë‹ˆë‹¤. ì„±ê³µ ìš”ê±´ì€ ERP/MRPÂ·MESÂ·ìˆ˜ìš”Â·ê³µê¸‰ì‚¬ ë¦¬ë“œíƒ€ì„/MOQÂ·í’ˆì§ˆ ë°ì´í„° í†µí•©, ë™ì  ì•ˆì „ì¬ê³ ì™€ ë¦¬ë“œíƒ€ì„ ë³€ë™ì„± ë°˜ì˜, ì˜ˆì™¸ ê·œì¹™Â·íœ´ë¨¼-ì¸-ë”-ë£¨í”„ ìŠ¹ì¸, ì¬ê³ Â·í˜„ê¸ˆ ì œì•½ ë° ê°ì‚¬ì¶”ì  ê°€ë“œë ˆì¼ ì ìš©ì…ë‹ˆë‹¤. ì‹¤í–‰ì€ Aë“±ê¸‰ í’ˆëª© íŒŒì¼ëŸ¿â†’ë²”ìœ„ í™•ëŒ€ë¡œ ì§„í–‰í•˜ê³ , ì„œë¹„ìŠ¤ë ˆë²¨Â·ê²°í’ˆì‹œê°„Â·ì¬ê³ íšŒì „Â·ìë™ë°œì£¼ ë¹„ìœ¨Â·ì˜ˆì¸¡ì •í™•ë„ ë“± KPIë¡œ ì„±ê³¼ë¥¼ ê´€ë¦¬í•˜ì„¸ìš”. By introducing AI agents for raw material reordering, through real-time data and predictions, you can proactively reduce stockouts and downtime, cut costs, and enhance decision-making through human-agent collaboration. Success requirements include: integration of ERP/MRP, MES, demand, supplier lead time/MOQ, and quality data; incorporation of dynamic safety stock and lead time variability; exception rules and human-in-the-loop approval; and the application of inventory/cash constraints and audit trail guardrails. Implementation should proceed from a pilot for A-class items to scope expansion, and performance should be managed with KPIs such as service level, stockout duration, inventory turnover, automatic ordering rate, and forecast accuracy.
ìš”ì•½:Summary:
ê¸°ì‚¬ì˜ í•µì‹¬ì€ ì›ìì¬ ì¬ì£¼ë¬¸ì„ ê·œì¹™ ê¸°ë°˜ì—ì„œ ì˜ˆì¸¡Â·ììœ¨ ì‹¤í–‰í•˜ëŠ” AI ì—ì´ì „íŠ¸ë¡œ ì „í™˜í•˜ë¼ëŠ” ì œì•ˆì…ë‹ˆë‹¤. ì œì¡° í˜„ì¥ì—ì„  ERP/MRPÂ·MESÂ·ìˆ˜ìš”Â·ê³µê¸‰ì‚¬ ë°ì´í„°ë¥¼ í†µí•©í•˜ê³ , ë™ì  ì•ˆì „ì¬ê³ Â·ë¦¬ë“œíƒ€ì„ ë³€ë™ì„±Â·ì˜ˆì™¸ì²˜ë¦¬Â·ìŠ¹ì¸ ì„ê³„ê°’ ë“± ê°€ë“œë ˆì¼ì„ ì„¤ê³„í•´ì•¼ í•©ë‹ˆë‹¤. Aë“±ê¸‰ í’ˆëª©ìœ¼ë¡œ ì‘ì€ íŒŒì¼ëŸ¿ì„ ì‹œì‘í•´ ë°ì´í„° ì •í•©ì„±ì„ ë†’ì´ê³ , KPI(ì„œë¹„ìŠ¤ë ˆë²¨Â·ì¬ê³ íšŒì „Â·ìë™ë°œì£¼ ë¹„ìœ¨ ë“±)ë¡œ íš¨ê³¼ë¥¼ ê³„ëŸ‰í™”í•˜ë©°, ì¶”í›„ ê³µê¸‰ì‚¬ API/EDIë¡œ í™•ì¥í•˜ëŠ” ë‹¨ê³„ì  ì ‘ê·¼ì´ ì‹¤ìš©ì ì…ë‹ˆë‹¤. ì´ë¥¼ í†µí•´ ê²°í’ˆê³¼ ê¸´ê¸‰ë¹„ìš©ì„ ì¤„ì´ê³ , ì¸ë ¥ì€ ì˜ˆì™¸ ê´€ë¦¬ì™€ ì „ëµ ì¡°ë‹¬ì— ì§‘ì¤‘í•  ìˆ˜ ìˆìŠµë‹ˆë‹¤. The core of the article is a proposal to switch raw material reordering from rule-based to a predictive, autonomously executing AI agent. In manufacturing sites, ERP/MRP, MES, demand, and supplier data must be integrated, and guardrails such as dynamic safety stock, lead time variability, exception handling, and approval thresholds must be designed. A phased approach is practical: starting a small pilot with A-class items to increase data consistency, quantifying effects with KPIs (such as service level, inventory turnover, and automatic ordering rate), and later expanding to supplier API/EDI. This will reduce stockouts and urgent costs, allowing personnel to focus on exception management and strategic procurement.

Global manufacturing investment flows have slowed due to tariffs and geopolitics, but capital is concentrating on AI infrastructure such as semiconductors and data centers, with mega-scale greenfield projects in the US and EU driving the investment amounts. Manufacturing AX should prioritize factory digital efficiency (OEE, yield, predictive maintenance) and energy cost hedging (PPA, on-site BESS) over large-scale new CAPEX, while simultaneously pipelining supply opportunities in the semiconductor/data center value chain (process equipment, power/thermal management, high-purity materials, cleanroom/modular solutions). In terms of location and funding, predicated on reshoring/incentive mapping, ROI and payback periods should be re-examined through blended finance combining government support, domestic PF, and ECA to increase feasibility in a 'higher for longer' environment.

While global investment in the first half of 2025 showed overall weakness, the overall investment value was sustained as capital concentrated on AI infrastructure (semiconductors, data centers). It is practical for manufacturers to accelerate ROI through AX (yield/quality/energy optimization) tailored to a high-interest rate environment rather than large-scale expansion, redesign their location and procurement strategies in line with US and EU incentives and the reshoring trend, and enhance investment execution capability with blended finance, such as domestic PF, ECA, and sovereign wealth funds.

í†µí•© ì¸ì‚¬ì´íŠ¸:Consolidated Insight:
ì¤‘ê³µì—…(ì² ê°•Â·ì‹œë©˜íŠ¸Â·ë¹„ë£Œ) ì¤‘ì‹¬ìœ¼ë¡œ ë°°ì¶œ ê´€ë¦¬ë¥¼ ê³µì¥ ë‹¨ìœ„ê°€ ì•„ë‹Œ ê¸€ë¡œë²Œ ì œí’ˆÂ·ê³µì • ë‹¨ìœ„ë¡œ ì „í™˜í•˜ê³ , ê·¸ë¦° í”„ë¦¬ë¯¸ì—„ì„ ë‚®ì¶”ëŠ” ê³µì •í˜ì‹  í¬íŠ¸í´ë¦¬ì˜¤(ìˆ˜ì†Œ-DRI, ì €ì—´ ì‹œë©˜íŠ¸/í´ë§ì»¤ ëŒ€ì²´, ì €íƒ„ì†Œ ì•”ëª¨ë‹ˆì•„)ë¥¼ íŒŒì¼ëŸ¿â€“ì‹¤ì¦â€“ì–‘ì‚° ë¡œë“œë§µê³¼ ê³µê¸‰ì‚¬ ê³µë™ê°œë°œ, MRV ì •í•©ì„±ìœ¼ë¡œ ì‹¤í–‰í•˜ì„¸ìš”. í•µë¶„ì—´/í•µìœµí•©ì˜ ì ì¬ë ¥ì€ ì¸ì •í•˜ë˜ ìƒìš©í™” ë¶ˆí™•ì‹¤ì„±ì„ ì „ì œë¡œ, PPAÂ·ì˜¨ì‚¬ì´íŠ¸ ë¬´íƒ„ì†Œ ì „ë ¥Â·ì¥ì£¼ê¸° ì €ì¥ì„ ì¡°í•©í•œ â€˜í™•ì‹¤í•œ ì „ë ¥â€™ ì „ëµì„ ìˆ˜ë¦½í•˜ê³  ì „ë ¥ë§ ì œì•½ì„ ë°˜ì˜í•œ ìƒì‚° ìŠ¤ì¼€ì¤„ë§ê³¼ ì—ë„ˆì§€ ë””ì§€í„¸ íŠ¸ìœˆìœ¼ë¡œ ìš´ì˜í•˜ì„¸ìš”. íƒ„ì†Œ ì œê±°ëŠ” ë³´ì¡° ìˆ˜ë‹¨ìœ¼ë¡œ ì œí•œí•˜ê³  íš¨ìœ¨Â·ì—°ë£Œì „í™˜Â·ê³µì • ë³€ê²½ì— ì§‘ì¤‘í•˜ë˜, AIë¡œ ì†Œì¬ íƒìƒ‰Â·ê³µì • ì œì–´Â·ì—ë„ˆì§€ ìµœì í™”ë¥¼ ê°€ì†í•˜ë©´ì„œ AI/ì „ê¸°í™”ë¡œ ì¸í•œ ì „ë ¥ ìˆ˜ìš” ì¦ê°€ë¥¼ ì¤‘ì¥ê¸° ì—ë„ˆì§€ ê³„íšì— ë°˜ì˜í•˜ì„¸ìš”. For heavy industries (steel, cement, fertilizer), transition emission management from a factory-unit basis to a global product and process-unit basis. Implement a process innovation portfolio (hydrogen-DRI, low-heat cement/clinker substitution, low-carbon ammonia) that lowers the green premium, through a pilot-demonstration-mass production roadmap, joint development with suppliers, and MRV consistency. While acknowledging the potential of nuclear fission/fusion, but under the premise of commercialization uncertainty, establish a 'reliable power' strategy combining PPA, on-site carbon-free power, and long-duration storage, and operate with production scheduling that reflects grid constraints and an energy digital twin. Limit carbon removal to a supplementary measure and focus on efficiency, fuel switching, and process changes. While accelerating material discovery, process control, and energy optimization with AI, reflect the increase in power demand due to AI/electrification in mid-to-long-term energy plans.
ìš”ì•½:Summary:
ê¸°ì‚¬ì˜ í•µì‹¬ì€ ì œì¡° íƒˆíƒ„ì†Œë¥¼ êµ­ê°€ ëª©í‘œê°€ ì•„ë‹Œ ê¸€ë¡œë²Œ ê³µì •Â·ì œí’ˆ ê´€ì ì—ì„œ ì ‘ê·¼í•˜ë¼ëŠ” ì „ëµ ì „í™˜ì…ë‹ˆë‹¤. ì² ê°•Â·ì‹œë©˜íŠ¸Â·ë¹„ë£Œì²˜ëŸ¼ ì „ ì„¸ê³„ì ìœ¼ë¡œ ê³µí†µëœ ê³µì •ì˜ ë°°ì¶œì„ ì¤„ì´ëŠ” í˜ì‹ (ê·¸ë¦° í”„ë¦¬ë¯¸ì—„ ë¹„ìš© ì ˆê°)ì´ ìš°ì„ ì´ë©°, í•µë¶„ì—´/í•µìœµí•© ë“± ì¥ê¸° ì €ë¹„ìš© ì „ë ¥ì˜ ê°€ëŠ¥ì„±ì„ ë³´ë˜ ìƒìš©í™” ë¶ˆí™•ì‹¤ì„±ì— ëŒ€ë¹„í•´ ê³ ì •Â·ë¬´íƒ„ì†Œ ì „ë ¥ì¡°ë‹¬ê³¼ ì „ë ¥ë§ ì œì•½ ê¸°ë°˜ì˜ ìš´ì˜ ìµœì í™”ë¥¼ ë³‘í–‰í•´ì•¼ í•©ë‹ˆë‹¤. íƒ„ì†Œ ì œê±° ê¸°ìˆ ì€ ë¹„ìš© ì¥ë²½ìœ¼ë¡œ ì¸í•´ ë³´ì¡°ì  ìˆ˜ë‹¨ìœ¼ë¡œ ë‘ê³ , AIë¥¼ R&D ê°€ì†Â·ê³µì • ì œì–´Â·ì—ë„ˆì§€ ìµœì í™”ì— í™œìš©í•˜ë©´ì„œë„ ì „ë ¥ ìˆ˜ìš” ì¦ê°€ë¥¼ ê³ ë ¤í•œ ì‹œë‚˜ë¦¬ì˜¤í˜• ì—ë„ˆì§€Â·ì„¤ë¹„ ê³„íšì„ ìˆ˜ë¦½í•˜ëŠ” ê²ƒì´ ì‹¤ì²œì  ì‹œì‚¬ì ì…ë‹ˆë‹¤. The core of the article is a strategic shift to approach manufacturing decarbonization not as a national goal, but from a global process and product perspective. Innovation that reduces emissions from globally common processes like steel, cement, and fertilizer (green premium cost reduction) is a priority. While considering the potential for long-term low-cost electricity such as nuclear fission/fusion, fixed, carbon-free power procurement and operational optimization based on grid constraints must be pursued in parallel in preparation for commercialization uncertainties. Carbon removal technologies should be kept as a supplementary means due to cost barriers. A practical implication is establishing scenario-based energy and facility plans that consider increased electricity demand, while utilizing AI for R&D acceleration, process control, and energy optimization.

IBM case studies prove the ROI of manufacturing AX with quantitative results such as JSR's quantum computing-based new semiconductor material and process optimization, Pfizer and OMV's approximately 20% cost improvement and end-to-end integration through governance/standardization, SIXT's 70% reduction in problem-solving time, and Avid Solutions' 25% reduction in onboarding time. Manufacturing sites can reduce downtime and digital project lead times by prioritizing the establishment of OT/IT integrated data platforms, AIOps-based equipment anomaly detection, self-service analytics and copilot, and SI standard templates and reference architectures (IaC/MLOps). However, as implementation details are limited, an approach combining internal pilots with benchmark metric setting is necessary.

This report systematically outlines the interactions between the EU AI Act and GDPR, Data Act, CRA, and NIS2. It states that manufacturers must distinguish between supplier/distributor roles and, by pre-classifying high-risk AI (such as safety components, quality inspection, and prediction), integrate NLF-based QMS, data governance, and logging/traceability. Particularly, for SMEs, jointly conducting FRIA/DPIA using a single template, reusing evidence such as logs, dataset sheets, and model cards, and designing CRA compliance (SBOM, vulnerability management, updates, linked to IEC 62443/ISO 27001), Data Act (IIoT data access, portability, FRAND, control over international transfers), and NIS2 (security risk management, incident reporting, model/API/cloud supply chain security) in an integrated manner reduces the burden. By establishing a 12â€“18 month roadmap tailored to the timeline of February 2025 (prohibited practices), August 2025 (GPAI, AI Office), and August 2026 (most provisions), and utilizing guidelines harmonized with regulatory sandboxes, the risks of entering the EU market and duplicate compliance can be minimized.

This content presents practical directions for directly applying the principles of Agentic/Ambient AI from medical settings to manufacturing, orchestrating people-processes-systems into a single flow, and implementing an integrated workflow connecting MES, ERP, CMMS, and QMS. It reduces the documentation burden for tasks such as shift meetings, inspections, and quality checklists through voice/on-site capture, and automatically triggers subsequent tasks (tickets, parts ordering, approvals), while being designed to meet GxP/ISO/IATF requirements through Human-in-the-Loop (HITL), audit trails, and risk-based phased automation. The application sequence is: selection of documentation/administrative-heavy processes â†’ ambient input â†’ agentic execution â†’ human review at quality/safety gates â†’ and verification of effectiveness with KPIs (documentation time, lead time, omissions/rework, audit findings).

Although it's a medical case, the core concepts of Agentic AI (human-centric assistance, automatic linkage between systems, trust based on human review, and frictionless integration with existing workflows) are directly applicable to manufacturing AX. The data presents the impact of China's semiconductor localization and procurement policies on manufacturing supply chains and market access, along with specific figures (50-70% localization for data center AI chips, 100% localization for automotive by 2027). It suggests hedging lead time and price risks by separating BOMs, certifications, and firmware for China-bound products and by using module/pin-compatible designs and dual-sourcing (including mature processes). In response to SOE procurement preferences, commercial cryptography regulations, and control over raw materials (such as germanium and graphite), restructure AVL/PPAP, conduct early cryptographic module conformity testing, and establish a system for material diversification and resource recovery. Assuming potential intensification from 2026-2030, it is practically effective to activate scenario planning, which includes a 'policy radar' and China+N / offshore computing and MLOps redundancy.

SIA's USTR submission indicates that policy tools such as China's semiconductor localization, subsidies, SOE procurement, standards, crypto regulations, and anti-dumping measures are promoting overcapacity and price wars in legacy chips and strengthening the exclusion of foreign chips in downstream industries such as data centers and automobiles. This is presented with specific figures and examples from local governments. Practical points from a manufacturing perspective are as follows: 1) Establishing a dual-track system for design, components, firmware, and certification for products destined for China, 2) Multi-sourcing, pre-qualification, and alternative component strategies for legacy chips, along with price/lead time hedging, 3) Reorganizing the Approved Vendor List (AVL) to reflect China's SOE procurement regulations, 4) A local conformity system in preparation for commercial crypto and security regulations, 5) A roadmap for replacement and re-resource utilization for material risks such as gallium and germanium, 6) Operating a 2026-2030 regulatory change radar and scenario planning, 7) Offshore capacity and MLOps redundancy to mitigate dependence on AI computing within China, 8) Risk diversification through China+N sourcing and localization. A substantial competitive advantage can be secured when policy and supply chain risks are managed in conjunction with an AX roadmap (design flexibility, supply chain digitalization, scenario-based decision-making).

Legacy OEMs' 'Frankenstein'-style heterogeneous software/ECU architecture increases production, testing, and quality risks due to the explosion of variants and integration difficulty. From a manufacturing AX perspective, productivity, quality, and service costs can be simultaneously improved by establishing a digital thread through the adoption of a single (integrated) software stack and domain/zonal E/E architecture, standard middleware/OS, standardization of factory EOL flashing and diagnostics (UDS/DoIP), an OTA-First process, and PLM-ALM-MES interconnection. Action points: Reduction in the number of ECUs/variants, securing SBOM and version traceability, expansion of SIL/HIL virtual verification, and managing release cycle, flashing success rate, and reduction in rework/recalls as KPIs.

The core of the article is that a 'single AI-centric software stack' is the key to competitiveness, and heterogeneous systems of legacy OEMs create limitations in terms of speed, quality, and cost. From a manufacturing perspective, a digital thread must be completed through ECU integration, standardization of diagnostics and flashing, an OTA-First process, and the linkage of PLM-ALM-MES. Considering the trend towards integrated stacks among BMW, Mercedes-Benz, and Chinese EV makers, transitioning the E/E architecture to domain/zonal and establishing CI/CD-factory linkage are practical implementation points.
